10.1016/j.jmp.2019.02.003
The set-theoretic framework of knowledge structure theory (KST;  Doignon & Falmagne, 1985, 1999; Falmagne & Doignon, 2011 ) offers various features that are highly desirable in psychological diagnostics. First, it provides a way to dispense with the a priori not plausible continuity assumption for representing the relevant psychological constructs, and establishes a discrete, non-numerical representation. Second, the theory is clearly multidimensional, and there is no need to fix the dimensionality of the representing structure in advance. Third, the approach avoids aggregating responses over items (as in forming a sum score), and thus can capitalize on the full information contained in the response pattern observed for an individual.

Knowledge structures, however, were developed for representing and assessing knowledge in prototypical domains such as mathematics, or chemistry, and they have most successfully been applied in these or related areas ( Falmagne, Albert, Doble, Eppstein, & Hu, 2013; Falmagne, Cosyn, Doignon, & Thiéry, 2006 ).  Falmagne (2015)  recently widened the scope to provide a framework suitable for medical and psychological diagnosis, and it turns out this generalization is exactly was is needed for interpreting KST as a psychological test theory. The following section elaborates on this issue, and introduces the generalized structures. Subsequently, the notion of a precedence relation between items as it is employed in KST is generalized accordingly. Building upon the introduced notions, the main theorem proved in the paper then provides the theoretical basis for a practical application of these generalized structures to data, as they are obtained in psychological testing based on questionnaires with dichotomous items. Application is illustrated for data on the Woodworth Psychoneurotic Inventory (WPI;  Papurt, 1930 ). The concluding discussion highlights communalities and distinctions of the presented theory and related KST-based approaches, and outlines possible directions for future research.

Doignon and Falmagne (1985)  define a  knowledge structure  as a pair  
                         
                            ( 
                            Q 
                            , 
                            K 
                            ) 
                         
                       in which  
                         Q 
                       is a nonempty set (assumed to be finite throughout the paper), and  
                         K 
                       is a family of subsets of  
                         Q 
                      , containing at least  
                         Q 
                       and the empty set  
                         0̸ 
                      . Here, the empty set  
                         0̸ 
                       refers to the naïve knowledge state, and  
                         Q 
                       refers to the state of complete mastery. The set  
                         Q 
                       is called the  domain  of the knowledge structure. Its elements are referred to as  items  and the subsets in the family  
                         K 
                       are labeled  (knowledge) states . A knowledge state represents the subset of items in the considered domain that an individual masters. More specific structural assumptions on the collection of knowledge states have received particular attention. Among them the  knowledge spaces  ( 
                         K 
                       closed under union), the  quasi ordinal knowledge spaces  ( 
                         K 
                       closed under union and intersection), as well as the  learning spaces , which are  well-graded  knowledge spaces (i.e., knowledge spaces in which for all knowledge states  
                         K 
                       distinct from  
                         Q 
                       there is an item  
                         q 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                         ∖ 
                         K 
                       such that  
                         K 
                         ∪ 
                         
                            { 
                            q 
                            } 
                         
                         ∈ 
                         K 
                      ).

In the sequel we will focus on the quasi ordinal knowledge spaces on a domain  
                         Q 
                      . Based on a celebrated result by  Birkhoff (1937)  it can be shown that they are in one-to-one correspondence to the quasi orders (i.e., reflexive and transitive binary relations) on  
                         Q 
                       ( Doignon & Falmagne, 1985 ). This leads to a straightforward interpretation of the set-theoretical structure in terms of a precedence relation among items. The quasi order implements the idea that certain pieces of knowledge can only be acquired after having learned some other pieces of knowledge. In a quasi ordinal knowledge space, the conclusions to be drawn from this precedence relation between items are twofold. First, if a knowledge state contains an item then all items preceding it are also contained in the state. Second, if a knowledge state does not contain an item then all items it precedes are not contained in the state, too.

Psychological and medical diagnosis is to be based on more general structures. Consider dichotomous items with possible responses like “yes”/“no”, or “agree”/“do not agree”. For each item the responses are coded by  
                         1 
                      , assigned to the response that scores the respective scale up, and by  
                         0 
                       otherwise. In the first case, we will say that the item is endorsed. In a scale intended to measure extraversion, for instance, the item “Do you make friends easily?” is endorsed by a “yes” response, the item “Do you have difficulties in making friends?” is endorsed by a “no” response. Then a knowledge state is to be interpreted as the subset of endorsed items. Given this interpretation, the requirement that the domain  
                         Q 
                       is a state (i.e., that all the items are endorsed) may not be plausible. There may not exist individuals exhibiting all of the symptoms captured by the items of a personality test, for example. Rather, there may exist various maximal states (w.r.t. set inclusion) which are to be interpreted as syndromes.

This leads to generalizing the notion of a knowledge structure.  Falmagne (2015)  recently introduced the concept of an assessment structure.

In a knowledge test, if an item is mastered we may infer that some other items are also mastered. In a personality questionnaire, however, it is reasonable to assume that whenever an item is endorsed we may not only infer that some other items are also endorsed, but, moreover, that still some others are  not  endorsed. If each item targets some symptoms, then from observing particular symptoms, we may be able to infer that some other symptoms are not present. In order to account for this type of dependency not yet covered in KST, the domain  
                         Q 
                       is extended by introducing positive as well as negative instances of the items. If item  
                         q 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                       is endorsed then this is represented by endorsing the positive instance  
                         
                            
                               q 
                            
                            
                               + 
                            
                         
                      , whereas not endorsing  
                         q 
                       is represented by endorsing the negative instance  
                         
                            
                               q 
                            
                            
                               − 
                            
                         
                      . Thus, for a given domain  
                         Q 
                       consider the set

                               Q 
                               ×

                                  + 
                                  , 
                                  − 
                                  } 
                               
                               , 
                            
                         
                      which is called the  extended item set  for  
                         Q 
                      . In the sequel, we will write

Now, consider a binary relation  
                         ≼ 
                       on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ±

Definition 3 
                         Let  
                               ≼

Notice that  (1)  arises from the fact that logically the inference  
                         
                            
                               t

                         ≼ 
                         t

Definition 4 
                         A binary relation  
                               ≼ 
                             on  
                               
                                  
                                     Q 
                                  
                                  
                                     ±

                                     Q 
                                  
                                  
                                     ±

                               ≼

                             is empty.

A binary relation  
                               ≼ 
                             on  
                               
                                  
                                     Q 
                                  
                                  
                                     ± 
                                  
                               
                             is said to be a  precedence relation on

                               ≼

                                     −

Restricting precedence relations to those consistent and self-reflecting quasi orders on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                       for which  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               + 
                            
                         
                         × 
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               − 
                            
                         
                       is empty excludes the cases where from not endorsing an item we can infer that some other item is endorsed. The rationale behind this constraint is that the presence of a symptom, but not its absence, may provide information on whether other symptoms occur, or not.

In analogy to the above mentioned result by  Birkhoff (1937)  a one-to-one correspondence can be established to characterize quasi ordinal assessment spaces. For convenience, the basic order-theoretic notions and results we draw upon in the sequel are provided in the  Appendix . The argument runs as follows. Given a domain  
                         Q 
                      , we first establish a Galois connection ( Definition A.1 ,  Appendix ) between the set  
                         R 
                       of all binary relations on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                      , and the set  
                         K 
                       of all collections of subsets of  
                         Q 
                      , both ordered by set inclusion. In a second step we characterize the respective closed elements ( Lemma A.1 
                      ii ,  Appendix ), which turn out to be the precedence relations on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                      
 ( Definition 4 ) and the quasi ordinal assessment spaces ( Definition 2 ). The closed elements are in one-to-one relationship due to  Lemma A.1 
                      iv .

Proposition 1  establishes the announced Galois connection.

With these results at hand it is not difficult to see that, whenever

                         K

In the sequel,  Theorem 1  is exploited to devise a procedure for empirically establishing quasi ordinal assessment spaces.

In KST various methods have been suggested to empirically determine a precedence relation on the set of items  
                         Q 
                       from available data, where each subject answered all items. See  Ünlü et al. (2013)  for an overview. Notice that we could pick any of these methods for our purpose. The following employs item tree analysis (ITA;  Schrepp, 1999; van Leeuwe, 1974 ) in a slightly modified way to establish a quasi order between items, giving rise to a quasi ordinal knowledge space on  
                         Q 
                      . We quickly review the application of this method on the domain  
                         Q 
                      , to which we will occasionally refer as the  standard case  or the  standard approach  below. Subsequently, we generalize the standard approach to treat precedence relations on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                      . ITA starts from considering the solution frequencies of pairs of items  
                         p 
                         , 
                         q 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                      , as listed in  Table 1 .

The intention of the analysis presented in this section is not to engage into a detailed psychological interpretation of the results, but to provide evidence that the theory developed above is indeed applicable to real data. This proof-of-concept study refers to data that were collected with an online version of the  Woodworth Psychoneurotic Inventory  (WPI;  Papurt, 1930 ). The WPI is commonly credited as the first personality test. It was developed for the United States Army during World War I to screen recruits for shell shock risk, and intends to measure emotional instability. The test consists of  
                         116 
                       yes/no-questions. The data are published and can be accessed online (retrieved from  https://openpsychometrics.org/_rawdata/ , 23 November 2017). Notice that the responses to items where a yes-response scores down the scale (e.g., items i 
                         30 
                      , i 
                         34 
                       in  Table 7 ) are recoded appropriately. There is a total of 6019 data sets available, including cases with missing values.

The subsequent analysis proceeds in two steps. First, it is examined whether a precedence relation (see  Definition 4 ) can be established for the data on the complete set of items. Second, because the large number of items prevents us from getting a complete picture of the quasi ordinal assessment space in this case ( 
                         
                            
                               2 
                            
                            
                               116 
                            
                         
                         ≈ 
                         8 
                         . 
                         3 
                         ⋅ 
                         1 
                         
                            
                               0 
                            
                            
                               34 
                            
                         
                       possible states), an in-depth analysis is performed for a set of selected items.

The following is based on the complete set of items, from which we eliminate item Q101  Did you ever have St Vitus’ dance? , which was endorsed by 33 subjects only (0.5 percent). Including this item (or, in general, items that were endorsed very rarely) would lead to non-consistency of the relation  
                            
                               
                                  ≼ 
                               
                               
                                  L 
                               
                            
                          even for comparatively small threshold values  
                            L 
                         . Excluding data sets with missing values at any of the remaining items, the data of  
                            3888 
                          subjects were included into the analysis.

First, the top left and the bottom right histograms in  Fig. 3  are identical, as are the entries in top left and the bottom right quadrants in  Table 6 .                             0̸

                         iii . Third, values in the bottom left quadrant are even smaller than those in the top left quadrant. Given an item is endorsed, inferring that another item is not endorsed is often possible with fewer exceptions than inferring that still another item is endorsed. Thus, the fact that their restrictions to  
                            
                               
                                  Q 
                               
                               
                                  −

To provide an example, let  
                            L

                            30

This means in particular that no inferences can be drawn from items that are not endorsed. Two items are preceded by remarkably many other items. If item i103 (see  Table 7 ) is endorsed then, according to  
                            
                               
                                  ≼ 
                               
                               
                                  L

For item i26 (see  Table 7  again) we can infer for 12 items that they are also endorsed, and for another 16 items that they are not endorsed. In both cases the preceding items are pairwise incomparable with respect to  
                            
                               
                                  ≼ 
                               
                               
                                  L 
                               
                            
                         .

The following will make the observations on the full test more concrete. The eight items listed in  Table 7  were selected to showcase the derivation of a quasi ordinal assessment space for the given data set. The appropriateness of the suggested theoretical extension for the considered data is illustrated by contrasting it to what one obtains from applying ITA within the standard approach. The results listed in  Table 9  are to be compared to those presented in  Table 8 . The quasi ordinal knowledge space resulting for

Here, however, not only the threshold value, but also the number of response patterns which are not states, as well as the number of states which are not response patterns, is by far larger. Thus, the quasi ordinal assessment space  
                            
                               ( 
                               Q 
                               , 
                               K 
                               )

Notice that, in contrast to the above reported results, considering a relation

                            192

                            a

                            b

Recently,  Falmagne (2015)  introduced assessment structures as a set-theoretic framework for medical, or psychological diagnosis by generalizing knowledge structures ( Doignon & Falmagne, 1985 ). The present paper develops a theoretical basis for a practical application of these generalized structures to the kind of data obtained in psychological testing based on questionnaires.

In contrast to knowledge structures, assessment structures need not contain the full set of items as a state, but only several maximal subsets of the item set. This reflects the fact that potential dependencies between items in these two applications are fundamentally different. In a knowledge test, if an item is mastered then, in principle, it is possible to infer that some other items are also mastered. Concerning a personality questionnaire, however, it is desirable to draw even more general inferences. Whenever an item is endorsed, it should be possible to not only infer that some other items are also endorsed, but, moreover, that still some others are  not  endorsed. This means that, from observing particular symptoms, we should be able to infer that some other symptoms are not present. In order to account for this additional type of dependency, the above development generalizes a celebrated result by  Birkhoff (1937) .  Theorem 1  establishes a one-to-one correspondence between a particular type of quasi orders (i.e., the precedence relations according to  Definition 4 ) on an extended item set  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                      , containing for each item a positive as well as a negative instance, and the so-called quasi ordinal assessment spaces ( Definition 2 ). This result has theoretical as well as practical implications.

On the theoretical side, the result makes clear that for characterizing a quasi ordinal assessment space  
                         
                            ( 
                            Q 
                            , 
                            K 
                            ) 
                         
                       with  
                         Q 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            
                               
                                  Q 
                               
                               
                                  1 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            … 
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  Q 
                               
                               
                                  i 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            … 
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  Q 
                               
                               
                                  n 
                               
                            
                            } 
                         
                       it is not sufficient to require that all of its substructures  
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               
                                  Q 
                               
                               
                                  i 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  K 
                               
                               
                                  i 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       form quasi ordinal knowledge spaces. With  
                         0̸ 
                         ∈ 
                         K 
                      , which is assumed to hold for all assessment structures ( Definition 1 ), it follows from  Proposition 3 
                      i.  that not only the  
                         
                            
                               K 
                            
                            
                               i 
                            
                         
                      , but also the structure  
                         K 
                       as a whole need to be closed under intersection. The assumption  
                         0̸ 
                         ∈ 
                         K 
                       does also put constraints on the types of dependencies that are captured by the precedence relation ( Definition 4 ), because it implies that its restriction to  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               + 
                            
                         
                         × 
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               − 
                            
                         
                       has to be empty ( Proposition 2 
                      v. ). This excludes the case that inferences on whether other items are endorsed can be made whenever an item (all of them formulated to score the scale up) is  not  endorsed. Differently put, if endorsing an item indicates that a particular symptom is present, then the absence of a symptom provides no information whatsoever on the presence of other symptoms. If inferences of the latter kind should be possible, then necessarily  
                         0̸ 
                       cannot be a state in the representing structure.

The practical significance of the result lies in the fact that it allows for constructing quasi ordinal assessment spaces from data. The procedure proposed in the present paper is a straightforward generalization of a previously suggested approach for building quasi ordinal knowledge spaces, known as ITA ( Schrepp, 1999; van Leeuwe, 1974 ). Analyzing data on the WPI ( Papurt, 1930 ) via this extended ITA produces relations  
                         
                            
                               ≼ 
                            
                            
                               L 
                            
                         
                       on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                      , which for a wide variety of threshold values  
                         L 
                       satisfy all the properties of a precedence relation on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                      . Cases in which not endorsing an item can be inferred from knowing that another item is endorsed seem to be a characteristic feature of the WPI. This demonstrates that the suggested generalization is needed for appropriately representing responses collected in questionnaire-based tests. As a consequence, the discrepancies between the observed response patterns and the derived states listed in  Tables 8 and 9  clearly favor quasi ordinal assessment spaces over quasi ordinal knowledge spaces.

Notice that approaches to establishing a precedence relation  
                         ≼ 
                       on  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                       are by no means limited to extended ITA. Other procedures to deriving such a relation from data (like  Sargin & Ünlü, 2009; Schrepp, 1999, 2003; Ünlü et al., 2013 ) may be generalized accordingly to operate on the extended item set  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                       rather than the domain  
                         Q 
                      . Notice also that  Falmagne, Cosyn, Doble, Doignon, and Spoto (2017)  propose a completely different approach to derive particular assessment structures, where the substructures according to  Definition 1  form learning spaces. The construction is based on applying a modified version of the QUERY routine ( Koppen & Doignon, 1990 ) to data. In contrast to the procedure developed above, this approach is not fully data-driven, and has to draw upon expert knowledge in the subject area for identifying the maximal states. The present results suggest that applying an appropriately generalized QUERY routine to the extended item set  
                         
                            
                               Q 
                            
                            
                               ± 
                            
                         
                       may offer a route to establishing this particular type of assessment structures in a completely data-driven way. Developing such a procedure is left for future work.

Data with missing values were discarded in the presented empirical application for convenience, but they may be taken into account in building the precedence relation on the basis of an available-case analysis. As long as responses are available for two items  
                         a 
                         , 
                         b 
                         ∈ 
                         Q 
                      , they can contribute to the contingency tables related to any pair from  
                         
                            
                               a 
                            
                            
                               + 
                            
                         
                         , 
                         
                            
                               a 
                            
                            
                               − 
                            
                         
                         , 
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               + 
                            
                         
                         , 
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               − 
                            
                         
                      , regardless of missing responses at other items. The situation is more involved concerning the goodness-of-fit, because only lower and upper bounds can be determined. An incomplete response pattern is to be compared to the projection of the assessment structure  
                         K 
                       to the subset  
                         P 
                         ⊆ 
                         Q 
                       of items that were answered (i.e.,  
                         K 
                         ∩ 
                         P 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            K 
                            ∩ 
                            P 
                            : 
                            K 
                            ∈ 
                            K 
                            } 
                         
                      ). This will yield lower bounds to the true distances, while adding the number of missing responses to these values will provide upper bounds. As we know of no systematic investigation of the effect of this uncertainty in the present setting (but see  de Chiusole, Stefanutti, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2015  on modeling missing data in probabilistic knowledge structures), for the time being it is recommended to base the computation of the goodness-of-fit on complete data sets only.

Having available tools for building assessment structures from data allows for taking advantage of the benefits of the underlying set-theoretic framework in psychological testing. Each individual is represented by a state, which formally is a subset of the set of items. This amounts to a very general multidimensional representation, which is both discrete and non-numeric. Special emphasis has to be given to the fact that the approach avoids aggregating responses over items (as in computing a sum score), and thus can capitalize on the full information contained in the response pattern observed for an individual. Moreover, its behavioristic perspective does not tie the approach to particular underlying cognitive process models. The approach, however, may nevertheless be able to handle their observable consequences. This is illustrated for an ideal-point model. Consider a minimal example  
                         Q 
                         = 
                         
                            { 
                            a 
                            , 
                            b 
                            , 
                            c 
                            } 
                         
                       of three items designed to measure introversion–extraversion. Assume that if item  
                         a 
                       is not endorsed then the person is highly introverted, and that endorsing item  
                         c 
                       means that the person is highly extraverted. Let  
                         b 
                       be a mid-range item such as “My social skills are about average”. It may be expected that the item is non-monotonic in the sense that it is not endorsed by highly introverted as well as highly extraverted persons. This is represented by the relations

                      , and leads to the precedence relation illustrated in  Fig. 7 (a), which in turn corresponds to the quasi ordinal assessment space depicted in  Fig. 7 (b).

The presented empirical application provides an example for the richness of the representation that may emerge. It shows that representing individuals by a subset

                       the set of maximal states, leads to a kind of overlapping classification. The maximal states  
                         
                            
                               Q

                       collect the largest combinations of symptoms that are to be observed. They may be conceived as syndromes which constitute extreme (maybe pathological) cases of a particular type. Any state

                       can then be seen as a less extreme case of this type. It is an empirical result, whether a state is identified to be of a single type only, or whether the classification is not unique. States lying in different substructures are to be distinguished, irrespective of whether they are of the same cardinality (giving rise to identical sum scores), or not. On the contrary, finding substantial overlap of substructures means that quite a number of items does not contribute to distinguishing between the different categories. Examples are the WPI items  
                         i 
                         30 
                       and  
                         i 
                         59 
                       denoted by  
                         c 
                       and  
                         e 
                       (both unrelated to the other selected items), but also WPI item  
                         i 
                         25 
                       denoted by  
                         a 
                       (included in all maximal states). Observations like this will be crucial for constructing tests based on the theory of assessment structures.

Giving psychological meaning to the syndromes represented by the maximal states amounts to relating them to diagnostic categories, for example, as they are documented in the Diagnostic and Statistical Manual of Mental Disorders (DSM-5;  American Psychiatric Association, 2013 ). This relates the presented theory to an approach for clinical–psychological diagnostics which was suggested by  Spoto, Stefanutti, and Vidotto (2010) . Drawing upon a skill-based extension (see  Heller, Ünlü, & Albert, 2013  for an overview) of KST, the so-called formal psychological assessment (FPA) assigns to each questionnaire item the diagnostic criteria (taken from DSM-5, for example) that it tests. Formally, this establishes a conjunctive skill function ( Heller et al., 2013 ) which induces a knowledge structure (that is closed for intersection) on the considered set of items. The fit of this knowledge structure to available data is then evaluated through application of the basic local independence model (BLIM;  Doignon & Falmagne, 1999 ), the standard probabilistic model within KST. Formally, FPA is closely related to a particular cognitive diagnostic model (CDM; e.g., see  DiBello & Stout, 2007; Rupp, Templin, & Henson, 2010 ), which is the Deterministic Inputs Noisy AND-gate model (DINA;  Haertel, 1984, 1989, 1990 ). See  Heller, Stefanutti, Anselmi, and Robusto (2015, 2016)  for details on the link between the two classes of models. In a series of papers (recent ones are  Serra, Spoto, Ghisi, & Vidotto, 2015; Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2016 ) FPA was applied to subscales of various psychological tests.

Taking a closer look reveals some differences between the suggested generalization of KST and FPA. First, while establishing a precedence relation on the extended item set intends to uncover the structure underlying the observed data, the structure postulated in FPA is derived from a purely theoretical analysis of the content of the items. The latter requires to extend the framework by introducing additional concepts. These are usually referred to as skills, or attributes, which are not part of the suggested theory (and so there is no direct link to CDMs). Thus, the approaches taken here and in FPA are complementary, and comparing the resulting structures may be highly informative. Second, FPA results in a knowledge structure which, as argued above, is too restrictive as a representation of data as they are obtained in psychological testing. Requiring the full set of items to be a plausible state limits its application, for example, to subscales of a test, which are usually established through statistical procedures (such as principal components analysis, or exploratory factor analysis) based on computing sum scores. The presented approach can be applied directly to the complete data set, and thus fulfills the promise to provide a truly non-numerical test theory. Third, drawing upon a probabilistic vs. deterministic version of the theory seems to form an obvious distinction between the approaches. However, a BLIM may be established on top of an assessment structure in exactly the same way as it is defined on a knowledge structure. The reason why we refrain from introducing a probabilistic framework into the present development lies in the fact that it comes with both benefits and drawbacks. On the positive side, it can account for states of widely varying probability, and for particular types of response errors, or response tendencies. On the negative side, the BLIM suffers from severe identifiability issues ( Heller, 2017; Spoto, Stefanutti, & Vidotto, 2012, 2013; Stefanutti, Heller, Anselmi, & Robusto, 2012 ), which often renders the interpretation of (at least some of) the parameter estimates impossible.  Heller et al. (2015, 2016)  provide a full characterization of the non-identifiability of the relevant probabilistic model FPA is based on (the so-called conjunctive CBLIM).

This discussion already makes clear that the generalization of KST presented in this paper may be extended in various directions. Assessment structures exclusively refer to observable responses. Bringing into the picture diagnostic categories, in a similar way as in FPA, allows for linking the data to the underlying psychological constructs or the relevant diagnostic categories that the assessment actually targets. Moreover, while being aware of the identifiability issues that may arise, putting the probabilistic framework of the BLIM on top of the assessment structures may still be useful. For example, it may reveal information on response tendencies, like endorsing an item without actually agreeing with it (simulating a symptom), or not endorsing an item while agreeing with it (dissimulating a symptom). The probabilities of these events form parameters of the BLIM. Procedures for parameter estimation in the BLIM were implemented in package  pks  ( Heller & Wickelmaier, 2013 ) of the statistical computing platform R ( R Core Team, 2016 ), and thus are readily available for application in this context. Finally, it is desirable to develop an analogous characterization of more general assessment structures, where, for example, the assumption of closedness under intersection is dropped. This calls for a generalization of results available for knowledge spaces (e.g., referring to the above mentioned QUERY routine) to assessment spaces, which may be achieved in very much the same way as the present paper generalizes from quasi ordinal knowledge spaces to quasi ordinal assessment spaces.

All this not only opens up an agenda for theoretical work, but also leaves us with the task of empirically exploring the potential of analyzing questionnaire data in psychological testing based on assessment structures.

The following two definitions introduce the basic order-theoretic notions of a Galois connection, and a closure operator.
